## markets

### market structure

#### integration

⟮Horizontal integration⟯ is the process of ⟮Controlling more of a specific part of the supply chain⟯
⟮Vertical integration⟯ is ⟮Integrating more/all or large parts of the supply chain in a single company⟯

## production & costs

factor of production ≈ input
The factors of production/inputs are what are necessary to produce the output(s) in production.
The production function describes relation between quantities of inputs and quantities of outputs.

### rent

Rent in economics is  payment to a owner of a factor of production/input in excess of what it costs the owner to bring the factor of production/input into the production process.
More specifically, economic rent can be thought of any payment for a factor of production/input that is itself not produced, such as land or assets formed by creating official privilege over natural opportunities (e.g., patents).
Rent-seeking is the atttempt to grow/maximize economic rent.
Since charging of rent does not increase productivity, rent-seeking has the effect of increasing one's share of wealth without contributing to productivity.

## costs

### opportunity

When doing a certain thing, that thing's ⟮opportunity cost⟯ is ⟮the loss of value/benefit⟯ from ⟮anything one could do instead (and has to forgo⟯)

### sunk

A ⟮sunk cost⟯ is a cost that ⟮has already been incurred and cannot be recovered⟯, in contrast, ⟮s1:2;a ⟮prospective cost⟯ is a cost that ⟮can be avoided if action is taken⟯⟯

### other types

flex-container:✫240px-CVP-TC-FC-VC.svg.png✫✫Marginal_cost1a.png✫


⟮Fixed costs⟯ ⟮are the costs that are not dependent on the quantity of goods or services provided⟯.
In contrast, ⟮variable costs⟯ ⟮increase as the quantity of goods or services produced/provided increase⟯.
⟮Together, fixed costs and variable costs make up⟯ ⟮total cost⟯, which is ⟮the cost of producing some quantity of output⟯.
The ⟮marginal cost⟯ of something is ⟮the cost of producing additional quantity⟯
⟮Marginal cost⟯ is either measured in ⟮one extra unit⟯, or in ⟮a graph for infinitesimal amounts⟯
The ⟮marginal cost⟯ is calculated by ⟮dividing⟯ ⟮the change in total cost⟯ ⟮by⟯ ⟮the change in total quantity⟯
The ⟮average cost⟯ of something is ⟮the total cost⟯ ⟮divided by⟯ ⟮the number of goods produced.⟯

### externalities

An ⟮externality⟯ is a ⟮c+;s3;cost⟯ ( = ⟮c+;s2;negative⟯ ⟮c+;s1;externality⟯) or ⟮c+;s3;benefit⟯ ( = ⟮c+;s2;positive⟯ ⟮c+;s1;externality⟯) caused by ⟮a producer⟯ that is ⟮not incurred or received by that producer.⟯

## economies

### economic sectors

#### three-sector model

TThe ⟮three-sector model⟯ divides economic activity into ⟮primary⟯, ⟮secondary⟯, and ⟮tertiary⟯ sectors.

sector|consists of
⟮Primary sector⟯|⟮Resource extraction = extraction of raw materials⟯
⟮Secondary sector⟯|⟮c+;Manufacturing = Raw materials → finished goods⟯
⟮Tertiary sector⟯|⟮Production of services⟯
⟮Quarternar sector⟯|⟮Production of knowledge⟯|not part of official model
⟮A society dominated in economic output by the (primary sector and) secondary sector⟯|⟮Industrial society⟯
⟮A society dominated in economic output by the primary sector⟯|⟮Pre-industrial/agrarian (more rarely agricultural) society⟯
⟮A society dominated in economic output by the tertiary sector⟯|⟮Post-industrial society⟯


According to ⟮the three-sector model⟯ of economics, economies develop ⟮Agrarian⟯ → ⟮Industrial⟯ → ⟮Postindustrial⟯

## goods and services

### price

#### asset price bubble

⟮Economic bubbles⟯ are about ⟮asset prices⟯.
In ⟮an economic bubble⟯, ⟮asset price⟯ ⟮far exeeds⟯ ⟮it's 'real' value⟯.
An economic bubble ⟮bursts⟯ when ⟮the asset price crashes.⟯
Both ⟮the growth⟯ and ⟮subsequent decline⟯ in asset prices in an economic bubble is usually ⟮quite fast⟯.

### goods

#### commodities

Commodity is a term with three competing definitions:
name|field|definition|examples
⟮commodity⟯|⟮economics⟯|⟮good with full/high degree of fungibility⟯|⟮c+;s∞;wheat, petroleum, copper, rice, but not handmade bracelets⟯
⟮commodity⟯|⟮marxism⟯|⟮something with both use and exchange value⟯
⟮commodity⟯|⟮casual usage⟯|⟮any kind of market good⟯

From these definitions, ⟮commodification/commoditization⟯ may refer to ⟮any of the three definitions of commodity (economics, marxism, casual usage⟯)

#### fungibility

If a good is ⟮fungible⟯, it is ⟮readily interchanged with others of its kind⟯ without ⟮that making much of a difference⟯.
⟮Money, natural resources of the same grade, stocks of the same company⟯ all are examples for things with a high degree of ⟮fungibility⟯.

#### distribution

##### positional goods

a ⟮positional good⟯ is a good that is ⟮only valued⟯ by ⟮how it is distributed in a population⟯.
⟮Positional good⟯ is a term similar to ⟮status symbol.⟯
  ⟮Conspicuous comsumption⟯ is consumption to ⟮enhance social status/prestige⟯, that is, consumption ⟮of goods⟯ as ⟮positional goods⟯

#### durability

A ⟮durable⟯ or ⟮hard⟯ good is a good that ⟮does not quickly wear out or, more specifically, one that yields utility over time rather than being completely consumed in one use⟯ (Examples: ⟮c+;s3;a bicycle, washing machine, or ring⟯ ).
A ⟮non-durable⟯ or ⟮soft⟯ good is either defined as ⟮a good that is either consumed in one use⟯ (Examples: ⟮c+;s7;food, drinks, tissues⟯ ) or ⟮a good that has a lifespan of less than three years⟯ (Examples: ⟮c+;s9;cosmetics, medication, clothing⟯).

#### excludability and rivalrousness

##### excludability

A good that is ⟮excludable⟯ ⟮can have its use limited to certain people⟯. Examples: ⟮c+;s2;cinemas, food⟯
examples for ⟮non-excludable⟯ goods: ⟮air, public roads⟯.
As argued by ⟮Ostrom⟯, in fact excludability is a ⟮scale⟯ ⟮from being able to fully limit people from its use to not at all.⟯

##### rivalrousness

A good is ⟮rival(rous⟯) if ⟮consumption by one party prevents consumption by another⟯
Examples for ⟮rivalrous goods⟯ include ⟮an item of food, a hammer, a domain name⟯.
A good is ⟮non-rival(rous⟯) if ⟮consumption by another induces no additional cost⟯
Examples for ⟮non-rivalrous goods⟯ ⟮a television broadcast, a scenic view, clean air⟯.
A good is ⟮anti-rival(rous⟯) if ⟮consumption by another provides benefits to other consumers⟯, generally via ⟮network effects⟯.
Examples of ⟮anti-rivalrous good⟯s include ⟮FOSS software, social media, a specific language⟯
We may want to define ⟮a scale⟯ ⟮from rival(rous) through non-rival(rous) to anti-rival(rous⟯).

A ⟮network effect⟯ is when ⟮the value of a good/service increases with the amount of users⟯. Examples: ⟮Social networks, the phone or the internet⟯.
The precise value of a ⟮network effect⟯ is often stated in terms of ⟮Metcalfe's law⟯: ⟮value of network⟯ = ⟮(amount of users)⎴2⎴⟯. This law has since recieved some ⟮empirical validation⟯ for e.g. ⟮facebook, bitcoin⟯

##### goods with properties

|⟮excludable⟯|⟮non-excludable⟯
⟮rivalrous⟯|c1;s1:4;private good⟯|⟮c+;s1:4;common(-pool) good⟯
⟮non-rivalrous⟯|⟮c+;s1:4;club/toll good⟯|⟮c+;s1:4;public good⟯

###### common pool resources

A ⟮common-pool resource⟯ is a good that is ⟮non-excludable⟯ and ⟮rivalrous⟯, e.g. ⟮pasture⟯.
A ⟮common-pool resource⟯ may be subject to ⟮the tragedy of the commons⟯ (however, not necessarily)
In ⟮the tragedy of the commons⟯, ⟮any individual⟯ has ⟮an incentive to⟯ {{c8::(over)exploit the resource}.
In ⟮the tragedy of the commons⟯, ⟮the resource is depleted⟯ due the overexploitation.
As proven by ⟮Elinor Ostrom⟯ in the work that won her ⟮the 2009 economics nobel prize⟯, ⟮commons/common-pool resources⟯ can ⟮effectively be managed by their communities⟯ if ⟮certain rules/principles are followed⟯, preventing ⟮the tragedy of the commons⟯

###### public goods

A good that is ⟮non-excludable⟯ (= ⟮use cannot be prevented⟯) and ⟮non-rival(rous⟯) (= ⟮use does not reduce availability⟯) is a ⟮public good⟯.
Examples of ⟮public goods⟯ are ⟮National security, lighthouses, knowledge⟯
⟮public goods⟯ are subject to the ⟮free rider⟯ problems, where ⟮free riders⟯ ⟮(c:6;people who use the good but don't contribute to it⟯) result in ⟮the good being under-produced or overused and degraded⟯.
Examples of public goods ⟮subject to free rider problems⟯: ⟮Herd immunity, grades of group-graded projects⟯
A ⟮free rider⟯ is someone who ⟮recieves a benefit⟯ ⟮without contributing adequately to its cost of production⟯.

###### excludable goods

A ⟮club good⟯ is a good that is ⟮excludable⟯ and ⟮non-rivalrous⟯, e.g. ⟮toll roads⟯
A ⟮private good⟯ is a good that is ⟮excludable⟯ and ⟮rivalrous⟯, e.g. ⟮cheese⟯


## behavioral economics

Behavioral economics studies the effects of psychological, cognitive, emotional, cultural and social factors on the decisions of individuals and institutions and how those decisions vary from those implied by classical economic theory.

### econs

⟮Econ⟯ is a term from ⟮behavioral economics⟯ which describes ⟮the beings⟯ that ⟮classical economics⟯ ends up describing
An ⟮econ⟯ would always ⟮make the best decision given a set of alternatives⟯
⟮Econs⟯, in contrast to humans, never fall prey to ⟮biases⟯
⟮Econs⟯ are contrasted with (actual) ⟮humans⟯

### nudge theory

Nudge theory proposes using nudges to influence the decision-making of groups or individuals to increase compliance with a goal.
A nudge is a minor, non-disruptive intervention (especially one that does not forbid or impose harsh sanctions) that produces predictable changes in people's behavior.
A fly at the bottom of an urinal to improve aim and reduce cleaning costs is a prime example of a nudge.

### bias

⟮Bias⟯ is the ⟮inclination toward⟯ or ⟮weighting of⟯ something, most often in a way that is ⟮unfair⟯ and/or ⟮disproportionate/wrong⟯. Thus, ⟮s1:3;⟮s7:12;⟮cognitive bias⟯ is ⟮the disproportionate weighting⟯ / ⟮irrelevant inclusion⟯ of factors in a way that ⟮the resulting judgement departs from rationality⟯⟯; ⟮s4-6,10-12;bias in ⟮statistics⟯ is the tendency of ⟮a statistic, more specifically an estimator (a truth about a sample⟯) to ⟮misestimate⟯ a ⟮parameter (a truth about the population) ⟯⟯;⟮s4:9; ⟮implicit bias⟯ are ⟮pre-reflective (or impossible-to-reflect-away) attitudes⟯ about ⟮a social group⟯⟯⟯ 
⟮Status quo bias⟯ is a form of ⟮cognitive bias⟯, the bias towards ⟮The current state of affairs⟯ 

#### cognitive bias

##### halo effect

The halo effect is the cognitive bias where positive impressions of something in one area  positively influences one's opinion about that thing in a different (generally unrelated) area

##### magical thinking

⟮magical thinking⟯ is the way of thinking that posits ⟮that two events are causally connected⟯, ⟮even though there is no plausible causal link⟯.
An example of ⟮magical thinking⟯ would be ⟮Knocking on wood will prevent misfortune.⟯ 
⟮Magical thinking⟯ is often ⟮a symptom of OCD⟯ (however, ⟮the cause of most⟯ ⟮magical thinking⟯ in the world is ⟮not⟯ ⟮OCD⟯)

##### mean world syndrome

Mean world syndrome is a hypothesized cognitive bias wherein people (especially those not particularly in danger of violence) may perceive the world to be more dangerous than it actually is, due to long-term moderate to heavy exposure to violence-related content on mass media.

##### decoy effect

An option C is asymmetrical dominated when it is inferior in all respects to one option A and a mix of inferior and superior to another option B.
In the decoy effect, there are three options A, B, and C, where C is assymerically dominated.
In the decoy effect, the decoy is the one that is asymetrically dominated.
In the decoy effect, people will choose the dominating option A more than if the decoy C was not present.

##### selection bias

###### WEIRD

⟮WEIRD⟯ is short for ⟮western⟯, ⟮educated⟯, ⟮industrialized⟯, ⟮rich⟯, and ⟮democratic⟯. 
⟮WEIRD⟯ is the set of traits which describes ⟮more than 90%⟯ of ⟮study participants⟯, especially in ⟮psychology⟯, being such, and thus results being ⟮biased⟯/⟮not generalizable⟯ 

## rational choice theory

⟮Rational choice theory⟯ is ⟮the area of study / framework⟯ that arises from the idea that ⟮an individual will⟯ ⟮perform a cost-benefit analysis (e.g. use an utility maximization function⟯) ⟮to choose an action⟯. 

### religious markets

⟮the idea of religious markets⟯ involves seeing religions ⟮through the lens/metaphor of a capitalist market⟯, most commonly one based on ⟮rational choice theory.⟯ ⟮hb;Thus, ⟮the market goods⟯ would be ⟮religions⟯, which would ⟮compete⟯ in such an environment.⟯ 
Analogous to the idea of ⟮religious markets⟯ ( = ⟮c+;s2,9;seing competition of religions through the metaphor of a market⟯ ) there is ⟮hb;the idea of the ⟮dating market⟯ ( = ⟮c+;s9;seeing competition between dating partners through the metaphor of a market) ⟯⟯ 

## unintended consequences

### godharts law

https://miro.medium.com/max/1400/0*u1dfHFPj2LZ41cAL.jpg

Goodhart’s Law is expressed simply as: “When a measure becomes a target, it ceases to be a good measure.”
Goodhart's law means that when we set one specific goal, people will tend to optimize for that objective regardless of the consequences.

### birth rate/fertility

The ⟮different fertility rates are⟯ sometimes presented ⟮as-is⟯ (as ⟮a percentage⟯), or ⟮multiplied by 1000⟯, to ⟮present a number/index⟯ 

What we often call ⟮birth rate⟯ is more properly called the ⟮crude birth rate⟯, because ⟮it isn't directly based on anything related to childbirth⟯ 
The ⟮crude birth rate⟯ is ⟮the number of live births⟯ ⟮per person⟯ 
To get ⟮the crude birth rate⟯ in practice, we ⟮divide the number of live births through the population size⟯ 

The ⟮general fertility rate⟯ is ⟮the amount of live births⟯ ⟮per women aged 15 to 44 (women of childbearing age⟯) 

The ⟮age-specific fertility rate⟯ is ⟮the number of births⟯ ⟮to women in a given age group⟯ ⟮divided by the number of women in a given age group⟯ 
The ⟮ASFR⟯ is often ⟮grouped in groups of⟯ ⟮1⟯ or ⟮5⟯ ⟮years⟯. 
In plain language, the ⟮ASFR⟯ e.g. for age 21 is ⟮the amount of children an average 21 y/o has in a year⟯ 

The ⟮TFR⟯ measures ⟮babies per woman (in her lifetime⟯) (*) 
Mathematically, the ⟮total fertility rate⟯ is ⟮the sum of all⟯ ⟮current age-specific fertility rates⟯ 
In plain language, the ⟮TFR⟯ is ⟮the sum of⟯ ⟮the amount of chilren⟯ ⟮a women will have⟯ ⟮in any given year of her life⟯ 
The thing the ⟮total fertility rate⟯ is ⟮trying to estimate⟯ is ⟮the amount of children a woman is expected to have in her lifetime (subject to some theoretical assumptions⟯) 
The ⟮total fertility rate⟯ assumes that ⟮the woman will survive until the end of her reproductive life⟯ and that ⟮she will to experience the exact current age-specific fertility rates (ASFRs) through her lifetime⟯ 
If a ⟮woman dies before her reproductive period ends (lets say at 30),⟯ she ⟮will not impact ASFRs beyond 30⟯. This is not a problem until ⟮we add them together to the TFR⟯ - since we are using the ASFRs to ⟮estimate how many children a woman will have in her life⟯, ⟮the ASFRs⟯ ⟮should be impacted b⟯⟮y the woman who died before the end of the reproductive period (the dead women should count as 0⟯). Therefore, ⟮maternal mortality distorts the TFR⟯. 


The ⟮replacement rate / replacement-level fertility⟯ is ⟮the TFR⟯ ⟮needed to sustain population levels⟯ 
A ⟮TFR⟯ ⟮below replacement rate⟯ is known as ⟮Sub-replacement fertility⟯ 
Beyond ⟮the distorting factors for the TFR⟯, ⟮the replacement rate⟯ is additionally distorted by ⟮child mortality⟯. 
⟮The replacement rate⟯ for countries ⟮with low child and maternal mortality⟯ is ⟮about 2.1⟯. 

Country|TFR
⟮Japan⟯|⟮~1.4⟯
⟮Korea⟯|⟮~0.9⟯

### dependency

#### definition of dependent

In demographics/economics, a ⟮dependent⟯ is someone ⟮who relies on someone else for financial support.⟯
For statistical purposes, it is generally assumed that ⟮those aged 15-64⟯ are ⟮non-dependents⟯, and ⟮those aged 0-14 and 65-⟯ are ⟮dependents⟯.
The relationship of ⟮dependents per non-dependent⟯ is expressed ⟮in the dependency ratio⟯.

#### dependency ratio

The ⟮dependency ratio (amount of people who work divided by people who can't⟯) is used as a proxy for ⟮pressure on the productive population⟯,
A ⟮lower⟯ ⟮dependency ratio⟯ is generally better because it often results in ⟮economic growth⟯.
When ⟮a change to a lower dependency ratio leads to economic growth⟯, which is most often caused when there is a ⟮high fertility rate⟯, resulting in ⟮a bunch of young people⟯, followed by ⟮a decrease in fertility⟯, and often ⟮augmented by an increase in life expectancy⟯, this is termed a ⟮demographic dividend⟯.
the idea of a ⟮demographic dividend⟯ is used to explain e.g. ⟮China's rapid economic growth⟯ - in ⟮2010⟯ they had an unprecedented ⟮dependency ratio⟯ of ⟮35%⟯, at the a time where the world average was ⟮53%⟯.
Of course, ⟮this large group of young people⟯ responsible for a ⟮demographic dividend⟯ will ⟮eventually grow old⟯, with ⟮less young people to support them⟯, leading to ⟮an increased dependency ratio⟯. this has been termed the ⟮demographic tax⟯ or ⟮demographic burden⟯.
A ⟮demographic burden/tax results⟯ in ⟮lower economic growth⟯ - a fact ⟮Japan⟯ ⟮is currently experiencing⟯ and ⟮China⟯ ⟮probably will fall victim to as well⟯.

### sex/gender

#### missing women

The term "⟮missing women⟯" (coined by ⟮Amartya Sen⟯) indicates a shortfall in ⟮the number of women⟯ relative to ⟮the expected number of women⟯ in ⟮a region or country⟯. 
⟮missing women⟯ are often measured/discovered through ⟮male-to-female sex ratios⟯, specifically via ⟮the deviance from⟯ the ⟮natural roughly 1:1 m:f ratio (in fact, in the west it is generally 0.98:1⟯) 
⟮Sen⟯ argued that there were about ⟮100 million⟯ ⟮"missing women"⟯ in ⟮Asia⟯. 
The phenomenon of ⟮missing women⟯ and is theorized to be caused by ⟮sex-selective abortions⟯, ⟮female infanticide⟯, and ⟮inadequate healthcare and nutrition for female children⟯. 
The fact of the existence of missing women is caused by ⟮sexism⟯, and may be seen as a pretty direct expression of ⟮their -value status.⟯ 

## marxist

### base and superstructure

base|forces and relations of production
superstructure|culture, institutions, rituals, states, etc.
In marxism, society consists of two parts, the base and the superstructure.
In marxism, the base and superstructure reciprocally influence each other, but the base is predominant.

### universal equivalent

To marx, ⟮the measure⟯ by which ⟮all commodities⟯ are ⟮compared and exchanged⟯ is known as  ⟮the universal equivalent.⟯
To marx, ⟮money⟯ is the thing that eventually takes over the role of ⟮the universal equivalent⟯ in capitalism.

### value

To ⟮marx⟯, the ⟮exchange value⟯ of a good is ⟮how it can be exchanged for other commodities⟯
To ⟮marx⟯, the ⟮use value⟯ of a good is ⟮its tangible features/physical properties⟯ that  ⟮fulfill human needs/wants⟯
form|corresp. value|explanation
⟮natural form⟯|⟮use value⟯|⟮actual tangible form (the actual physical form, e.g. cloth, iron, etc.⟯)
⟮value form⟯|⟮exchange value⟯|⟮a good in relation to different commodities (e.g. 20 yards of linen = 1 coat⟯)

### labor

#### reproductive labor

reproductive labor is the work that reproduces other labor.
Reproductive labor is absolutely necessary - without reproductive labor, there would be no labor at all.
Reproductive labor consists of chores and childrearing.

## areas

### real estate

#### condominium

# political science

## political spectrum

### overton window

flex-container:✫sm_paste-d320cd7f7b28b6755889aacc46fa08635d6a2262.jpg✫
⟮The overton window⟯ is the concept that there is ⟮a range of ideas/policies⟯ that are ⟮acceptable⟯ at ⟮any given time⟯, which then ⟮determines the acceptability/radicalness of other ideas⟯ ⟮in relation to it⟯. 
In the overton window concept, everything ⟮inside the window⟯ is considered to be ⟮acceptable/reasonable/sensible⟯ 
In the overton window concept, everything ⟮outside the window⟯ is considered to be ⟮radical/unthinkable⟯ 
⟮Introducing radical ideas⟯ ⟮shifts the overton windon into that direction⟯. 
To make something ⟮viable/actual policy⟯, first ⟮the overton window⟯ needs to be ⟮shifted in that direction.⟯ 

### horseshoe theory

flex-container:✫polspechorse.svg✫

Proponents of ⟮horseshoe⟯ theory argue that the ⟮extreme left⟯ and the ⟮extreme right⟯ are ⟮closer to each other than either is to the political center.⟯  
⟮Horseshoe⟯ theory is probably mostly ridiculous, since it's may generally be ⟮centrists⟯ who ⟮easily become swayed to support the rise of fascism⟯ 

## international relations

### diplomacy

#### checkbook diplomacy

Checkbook diplomacy is using economic aid and investment for diplomatic favor.

### irredentism & revanchism

⟮Irredentism⟯ is the movement to ⟮'reclaim' territory⟯ that a group thinks ⟮they have a claim to⟯ on a ⟮ethno-historico-nationalist⟯ basis. 
⟮Revanchism⟯ is the movement to ⟮reclaim territory⟯ ⟮lost to another⟯ due to ⟮past events⟯. 
Both ⟮irredentism⟯ and ⟮revanchism⟯ see their ⟮territorial claims⟯ as a ⟮reclaiming⟯, though the ⟮motivation/ideology behind it⟯ differs. 
⟮revanchism⟯ is like ⟮irredentism⟯, but generally the territory claimed ⟮was once actually held⟯, with less emphasis on ⟮whether that territory is ethnically/'properly' part of the nation⟯ 


France ⟮claiming alsace-lorraine/wanting it back⟯ was both a ⟮irredentist⟯ and a ⟮revanchist⟯ claim. 
An ⟮irredentist⟯ claim is often expressed as a claim for ⟮Greater X (Greater Serbia etc.⟯) 
the ⟮Megali Idea (Great Idea⟯) was an ⟮irredentist (though probably not revanchist⟯) claim by ⟮greece⟯ 
The term ⟮irredentism⟯ comes from ⟮italia irredenta⟯ (= ⟮irredentism⟯ related ⟮to italians in dalmatia, corsica, etc.⟯) 
The etymological roots of irredentism is that the territory is ⟮not redeemed (from italian Italia irredenta⟯)

### structure

flex-container:✫sm_2021-06-27--09-51-54-screenshot.jpg✫
Someone ⟮doing something⟯ defined as ⟮the intended result⟯ of ⟮an ad or similar⟯ is known as a ⟮conversion⟯. 
⟮A purchase, a click on a certain thing, subscribing to a newsletter⟯ might all be ⟮Conversions⟯ 
The ⟮conversion rate⟯ measures ⟮conversions⟯ per ⟮site visitor (or similar⟯) 
A ⟮sales/conversion funnel⟯ is the process where you repeatedly ⟮reduce the users options⟯ until ⟮a conversion⟯. 
A ⟮call to action (CTA⟯) is something that is ⟮designed to⟯ ⟮prompt a a conversion⟯. 
In general, ⟮the CTA⟯ should be the thing with ⟮the most visual focus⟯. 
In web design, ⟮a call to action⟯ is most often ⟮some kind of button⟯. 
The thing that ⟮gets people to the next step⟯ of ⟮the sales/conversion funnel⟯ is ⟮a CTA⟯. 

### manner

#### astroturfing

⟮Astroturfing⟯ is the practice of making ⟮advertisment of a product or message⟯ appear as though ⟮it originates from and is supported by grassroots participants⟯. 

#### brigading

⟮Brigading⟯ is when ⟮a large number of users⟯ ⟮coordinate⟯ to ⟮give a (generally negative) rating to something⟯, or ⟮to vote in an online poll,⟯ or similar. 
The goal of ⟮brigading⟯ is often either ⟮to harm the thing in question⟯, or ⟮to give the idea of the genuine opinion of something being different than it  actually is⟯. 
⟮Brigading⟯ may in a wider sense also refer to ⟮all coordinated harmful online action⟯. 

#### decieving

##### sockpuppet

A ⟮sockpuppet⟯ is ⟮an alternative account⟯ used to ⟮decieve or mislead in some way⟯.⟮s1:3;Originally this referred to ⟮an account to talk about or to oneself while pretending to be another person⟯, ⟮s4;then expanded to ⟮all uses of a second account pretending to be a second person while being the same as the first⟯, and now can also refer to ⟮s5;⟮any (misleading) activity where a second account might be helpful⟯, such as ⟮evading bans⟯, do ⟮mass action such as brigading⟯, etc⟯⟯.⟯ 

### message

#### misleading, hiding, deciebing

##### dogwhistle

A ⟮dog whistle⟯ as a physical thing is ⟮a whistle that can be heard by dogs but not humans⟯. 
By extension, a ⟮dog whistle⟯ in politics is (the use of) ⟮coded language⟯ which ⟮sounds innocent to most people⟯ (or ⟮at least allows plausible deniability⟯) but ⟮is meant to communicate a specific thing to an intended audience⟯. 
Examples for ⟮dog whistles⟯ are ⟮"international bankers"⟯ to mean ⟮jews⟯, or ⟮states' rights⟯ as code for ⟮segregation/racism/pro-slavery⟯ 

##### weasel words

A weasel word (better: weasel phrase) is a word or phrase used to create the impression that a strong claim has been made, when in fact only a vague and thus weak claim has been made.
Most commonly weasel words/phrases are created via vague or weakening quantifiers, unclarity about referents, use of the passive voice.
Examples of weasel words:
- A growing body of evidence suggests (Unclarity about referents - what evidence?)
- People are saying/Critics claim/... (Unclarity about referents - who?)
- It is recommended that (Passive voice - who is recommending?)
- [It is] officially known as (Passive voice - what is the official body here?)
- Often, x may (Vague or weaking quantifier - in which circumstances?)

##### bothsidesism

⟮Bothsidesing⟯ is a term where you ⟮present both sides of an argument with equal weight⟯ (or ⟮assign both parties equal fault⟯), even if ⟮one does actually merits more weight⟯ (e.g. ⟮is true, is more representative⟯) or ⟮have more fault⟯ 
⟮Bothesidesing⟯ as a term is generally used as ⟮a criticsim⟯, especially ⟮of the media.⟯ 

##### washing

xwashing is a snowclone (I think) indicating manipulation of appearances/public opinion that a certain thing (mostly product) is x-supportive, often with the implication that the support for the issue symbolized by x is only surface-deep.
examples of x-washing: greenwashing (environmentalism), pinkwashing (LGBTQ)
xwashing stems from whitewashing.
whitewashing originally meant the application of whitewash (a lime and water mixture) to paint walls and fences bright white.
whitewashing was then extended to mean papering over unfavorable elements.
Finally, whitewashing has come to mean making something more palletable to white people, esp. by casting POC as white people.

### freedom of speech

#### suppression of speech

##### impressumspflicht

⟮Impressumspflicht⟯ is generally only a thing in ⟮german-speaking countries⟯, containing ⟮the contact information⟯ of ⟮the person responsible for the content.⟯ 
The main law ⟮requiring an Impressum⟯ in the Internet is ⟮the Telemediengesetz⟯, if ⟮you are offering services⟯, also ⟮the  Dienstleistungs-Informationspflichten-Verordnung⟯ 
The main requirement for an ⟮impressum⟯ is "⟮leicht erkennbar⟯, ⟮unmittelbar erreichbar,⟯ ⟮ständig verfügbar⟯" 
^Historically, the German impressum requirement has its roots in the censorship laws of 19th century and earlier monarchies, and has been criticized as illiberal and contrary to the principle of free speech; in most other countries there is no comparable requirement.

##### chilling effect

A chilling effect is an effect that reduces or supresses the exercise of rights or the expression of speech, mostly by self-policing.

#### paradox of tolerance

The paradox of tolerance states that if a society is tolerant without limit, its ability to be tolerant is eventually seized or destroyed by the intolerant.
The paradox of tolerance was introduced by Karl Popper in a note to his 1945 the open society and its enemies.

### place / time

#### US

In American politics, the ⟮Southern strategy⟯ was a ⟮Republican Party⟯ electoral strategy to increase political support ⟮among white voters in the South⟯ by ⟮appealing to racism against African Americans⟯ starting during (and exploiting the fears created by⟮ the civil rights era of the 1950s and 1960s⟯), causing the ⟮s2;Republican party⟯ to end up ⟮far further to the right and in roughly the same position as it has today⟯. Politicians most associated with this are ⟮Richard Nixon⟯ and ⟮Barry Goldwater.⟯ 

⟮Lee Atwater⟯ has a famous ⟮quote⟯ in which he describes the evolution of ⟮republican::whose?⟯ messaging in the context of the ⟮southern strategy⟯ from ⟮"Nigger"⟯ to ⟮dog-whistles⟯ such as ⟮forced busing⟯ and ⟮state's rights⟯ to ⟮supposedly economic issues⟯ such as ⟮cutting taxes⟯ that ⟮"just happen" to hurt black people more⟯. 
⟮Lee Atwater'⟯s famous quote is often understood as ⟮the intentional perfidiousness of hiding racism in structural violence not mentioning race⟯, but he actually believed that ⟮this would end up making race less important⟯. 

> You start out in 1954 by saying, "Nigger, nigger, nigger." By 1968, you can't say "nigger" – that hurts you. Backfires. So you say stuff like forced busing, states' rights and all that stuff. You're getting so abstract now, you're talking about cutting taxes. And all these things you're talking about are totally economic things and a byproduct of them is [that] blacks get hurt worse than whites. And subconsciously maybe that is part of it. I'm not saying that. But I'm saying that if it is getting that abstract, and that coded, that we are doing away with the racial problem one way or the other. You follow me – because obviously sitting around saying, "We want to cut this," is much more abstract than even the busing thing, and a hell of a lot more abstract than "Nigger, nigger."

ARPA is short for ⟮Advanced Research Projects Agency⟯ (today called ⟮DARPA⟯), and is an agency that carries out ⟮c+;R＆D⟯, directly subsidiary to the ⟮US Department of Defense⟯

### hermeneutics

#### hermeneutic circle

flex-container:✫image7.jpg✫
The ⟮hermeneutic circle⟯, across it's varied definitions, refers to the idea that ⟮understanding⟯ of ⟮a text/thing⟯ consists of ⟮moving between⟯ ⟮two things/states⟯. 
In the hermeneutic circle, ⟮moving between the two states⟯ ⟮deepens or at least changes⟯ our ⟮understanding⟯. 
For the hermeneutic circle, since ⟮our understanding at either step⟯ is ⟮never exactly the same⟯ ("⟮No person ever steps in the same river twice⟯"), it may be more apt to call it a ⟮spiral⟯. 
⟮hermeneutic circle⟯|⟮gadamer⟯|⟮what I expect the text to say/something to be⟯|⟮what I read in the text/experience something to be⟯
⟮hermeneutic circle⟯|⟮???⟯|⟮what I know⟯|⟮What I read in the text⟯
⟮hermeneutic circle⟯|⟮Schleiermacher⟯|⟮part of the text⟯|⟮the whole text⟯

#### molloy pebble scene

In the pebble scene, the protagonist wants to ⟮suck all 16 pebbles⟯ without ⟮repeating one⟯

###### harlequin

⟮h∞;✫sm_51VaV4puTsL._SX297_BO1,204,203,200_.jpg✫
⟮Harlequin Enterprises⟯ is a company that ⟮publishes romance novels⟯, and reached a peak market domination (in NA) of ⟮80-90%⟯ of sales in ⟮the 1990s⟯. As of 2002, they publish about a ⟮1k books⟯ a year.
⟮Harlequin⟯ sets ⟮precise standards⟯ for their romance novels, with ⟮restrictive contracts⟯ with ⟮bad pay⟯ and ⟮precise page counts⟯. 
⟮Harlequin⟯ features different ⟮series⟯ with different ⟮themes⟯, which also vary things such as ⟮sexual content⟯. 
Harlequin series include 『⟮Love Inspired⟯』 ⟮(c:15;Christian romances where faith is featured as prominently as passion⟯) and 『⟮Harlequin Presents⟯』 ("a⟮lpha males, decadent glamour and jet-set lifestyles⟯") 
According to Radway (1984), ⟮the harlequin hero⟯ is s⟮pectacularly masculine, well-built, etc.⟯ in appearance, ⟮indifferent/bad-tempered⟯ in personality, but ⟮with a token indication of underlying softness (e.g. love of animals or children⟯) 
According to Radway (1984), the ⟮harlequin heroine⟯ is ⟮young⟯,  has ⟮extraordinary but unselfconscious beauty⟯, and is ⟮inexperienced⟯ in love. 
Harlequin romances generally feature a ⟮clinch⟯ cover and have a ⟮happy⟯ end. 

###### japanese

According to Shibamoto Smith 2004, in contrast to ⟮Harlequin translations⟯, the ⟮heroine⟯ of ⟮japanese romance novels⟯ looks ⟮reasonably pretty, but not a bombshell⟯, acts ⟮independently/competently⟯, is quite ⟮self-aware⟯, while the ⟮hero⟯ is ⟮ordinary, nice, somewhat awkward⟯. 
In general, according to Shibamoto Smith 2004, ⟮japanese romance novels⟯ pay less heed to ⟮looks as a sign for attractiveness/love⟯ 
> Japanese romances rarely have covers that depict the principal characters, and the text descriptions are by no means as detailed as in the Harlequin-style texts. Clothing is not, in my corpus, ever used as a tool of seduction, and no lovers’ furtive glances at escaping chest hair or heaving breasts or quick but intoxicating looks at glimpses of beautiful long legs through the slits of skirts help our Japanese lovers know that they have found Ms. or Mr. Right

### journalism

#### newswriting

##### structure

The ⟮introductory section/opening paragraph⟯ is known as ⟮the lede⟯, especially in ⟮news writing⟯.  
In general, it is said the lede ⟮should contain⟯ ⟮the most newsworthy/interesting part⟯, and ⟮sb;⟮if an article does not have the most newsworthy part in the lede⟯, it is said to be ⟮burying the lede⟯⟯. 
If we're reporting a house fire, and we mention the location, time and cause of the fire before mentioning the two deaths, what are we probably doing?  ⟮Burying the lede⟯ 
Nora Ephron had an assignment to write a ⁑opening⁑ for a news story about t⁑eachers going to speak⁑ in a neighboring city on ⁑thursday⁑ for the ⁑school paper⁑. What (according to her teacher) is the ⁑lede⁑ for this story?  ⟮There will be no school next thursday.⟯ 
A doctor is giving a speech about fad diets and physical fitness to a group of local businesspeople. Midway through his speech, the good doctor collapses of a heart attack. He dies en route to the hospital. What should definitely be  in the lede? ⟮the death (and prob the heartattack⟯)

flex-container:✫sm_giphy-f628103d57d332cf39ba9c4be4ef5a7d43ae20a7.gif✫✫sm_hqdefault-b5ad12cf8a7b07c83685f3fb5033582023454ef3.jpg✫✫sm_nicegai.jpg✫✫sm_1000.jpg✫✫sm_paste-bef82170e18bf7573ea1aeaf93edfa0d17dd0905.jpg✫

##### screenplay

⟮Fountain⟯ is  a markup language for ⟮screenplays⟯ inspired by ⟮markdown⟯ 

###### title page

In fountain, a ⟮title page⟯ consists of ⟮key-values pairs⟯, ⟮hb;separated internally by ⟮:⟯.⟯ 
In fountain's title page you can have ⟮multiple of the same key⟯. the value can ⟮stretch over multiple lines⟯, ⟮hb;if it does, ⟮lines beyond the first⟯ should be ⟮preceded by 3 or more spaces, or a tab⟯. ⟯ 
In fountain, the ⟮title page⟯ is optional, but must be ⟮the first thing in the document if it appears⟯. 

###### parantheticals

In screenplays, ⟮parantheticals⟯ are ⟮the annotations in parentheses⟯, generally ⟮between⟯ ⟮a characters name⟯ and ⟮their dialogue⟯. 
⟮parathenticals⟯ are ⟮centered⟯, as are ⟮the character name⟯, and ⟮the dialogue after.⟯ 
In screenplays, ⟮characters names⟯ are written in ⟮all caps⟯ when ⟮above dialogue elements⟯ and when ⟮they first appear⟯, and ⟮in normal case afterwards⟯. 
In fountain, an ⟮all-caps line⟯ automatically becomes ⟮a charater element⟯ ⟮(c:16;a character name before dialogue⟯) unless ⟮it has a good reason not to⟯. 
In fountain, you indicate ⟮a paranthetical⟯ by ⟮surrounding it in (⟯). 
In fountain, anything ⟮after a paranthetical element⟯ or ⟮after a character element⟯ ⟮automatically becomes dialogue⟯. 
In fountain. ⟮a caret⟯ ⟮after a character name⟯ indicates that ⟮this is dialogue simultaneous/dual/parallel⟯. 

###### action text

flex-container:⟮h∞;✫sm_paste-93acbcc29329144602ed62e027beb9100c001b91.jpg✫⟯

In screenplays,  ⟮text describing what is happening in a scene⟯ is called ⟮action (text⟯). it is normally in ⟮normal case.⟯
⟮Any text that fountain doesn't think is something else⟯ is ⟮action text⟯.  
Within ⟮action text⟯, fountain ⟮preserves your whitespace choices⟯ 

###### scene headings

The ⟮line⟯ ⟮starting a scene⟯ is called ⟮a scene heading⟯. 
A ⟮scene heading⟯ is usually in ⟮all caps⟯. 
⟮Scene headings⟯ tell you ⟮outsideness⟯, ⟮location⟯, and ⟮time⟯. 

A ⟮scene heading⟯ usually looks sth like this:
```lang=text;
⟮EXT or INT etc.⟯⟮.⟯ ⟮LOCATION⟯ ⟮-⟯ ⟮TIME⟯
```
In fountain, ⟮scene headings⟯ are ⟮separated from the rest of the text⟯ by ⟮blank lines⟯. 
In fountain scene headings ⟮must start with⟯ ⟮EXT/INT etc.⟯ unless forced 
In fountain, you can indicate ⟮scene numbers⟯ by ⟮wrapping them in #foo#⟯, they are placed at ⟮the end of scene headings⟯

###### transitions

flex-container:✫6a0105358b73d4970c0133f551c0ae970b-pi.jpg✫


In screenplays, ⟮transitions⟯ are in  ⟮all caps⟯ and ⟮right-aligned⟯.
In fountain, a ⟮transition⟯ must be ⟮separated from other content⟯ by ⟮blank lines⟯, and end ⟮TO: (unless forced⟯)

#### kinship

The ⟮terminology we use to describe⟯ ⟮kinship relationships⟯ is known as ⟮kinship term(inology⟯)

# urban studies

## community

### third place

first place|home
second place|work

A third place is a place in a humans life outside of the first/second place where they spend time and socialize.
The idea of third places was coined by Ray Oldenburg.
Ray Oldenburg argues that third places are important for individuals, communities and democractic/civic society.

#### characteristics of a third place according to theorists

At least for regulars, third places are not particularly restricted in access, and people have little to no obligation to be there.
Third places must feel warm, accomodating and accepting of different people, with little distinction and snobbery.
Third places have a certain set of regulars, though those are not necessarily the only people in the space.

## tools

### cardinal directions & compasses


flex-container:✫sm_italy-map-with-compass-rose-vector-id165747631.jpg✫✫sm_1280px-Windrose.svg.jpg✫


A ⟮compass rose⟯ can be found on ⟮pretty much any compass⟯ and on ⟮many maps⟯. 
The ⟮compas rose⟯ indicates ⟮where the cardinal directions are⟯, ⟮in relation to⟯ ⟮the thing on which it appears⟯. 
The ⟮four⟯ ⟮main directions⟯, ⟮90 deg⟯ apart, that a compass rose has are ⟮the cardinal directions⟯. 
The ⟮four⟯ directions e⟮xactly in the middle between the cardinal directions⟯ are the ⟮intercardinal directions⟯. 
The ⟮intercardinal directions⟯ have the syntax ⟮‹north/south›‹east/west›⟯ 
The ⟮eight directions between the intercardinal directions⟯ I will term ⟮interintercardinal directions⟯. 
The ⟮interintercardinal directions⟯ have the syntax ⟮‹cardinal-direction›‹intercardinal-direction›⟯&nbsp;, where the intercardinal direction and the cardinal are ⟮those they are closest to⟯. 
The sixteen directions ⟮between the interintercardinal directions⟯ I wil term ⟮interinterintercardinal directions⟯. 
Generating the ⟮interinterintercardinal directions⟯ follows the following algorithm:
```
let iiicString = ⟮iic.closest(cardinal or intercardinal direcction⟯)
⟮iicString += "by"⟯
if ⟮iic.isClockwiseOf(closest(cardinal or intercardinal direcction⟯))
  ⟮iicString += closestClockwise(cardinal direction⟯)
else 
  ⟮iicString += closesCounterclockwise(cardinal direction⟯)
```

A bight at its most general is just any kind of curve anywhere.
A bight in coastal geography is a curve in a coastline/river that is only slightly recessed (ergo broad), open/unobstructed, and shallow.

## integrated geography

### disasters

While it might seem obvious to ⟮evacuate⟯ in the face of a storm, it may be ⟮difficult⟯ due to ⟮traffic blockages⟯ or ⟮economic considerations⟯ 
Since ⟮you may not be able to wash things for a while⟯ once a storm (e.g. hurricane, severe flooding, etc.) hits, ⟮wash/clean everything you can beforehand⟯ 
Since ⟮ATMs and electronic payment may not work⟯, having ⟮enough cash⟯ is important in an emergency situation 
Regardless of where you end up ⟮weathering a storm⟯, it's good to ⟮pack distractions⟯, especially ⟮non-electronic⟯ ones (or ⟮powerbanks⟯ etc.), to ⟮stave off boredom⟯ and ⟮keep stress-levels down⟯ 
Only use ⟮generators⟯ ⟮outside⟯ and ⟮away from⟯ your home/the space you are staying. 
One of the ⟮first things⟯ to do if ⟮you find out that a disaster will hit⟯ is ⟮find out if you should evacuate⟯. 
Many countries will have predefined ⟮evacuation routes⟯ for evacuation, which you ⟮should follow if possible⟯ 
In the US at least, for storms such as hurricanes there are ⟮voluntary⟯ and ⟮mandatory⟯ ⟮evacuation orders⟯. 
In response to an ⟮emergency⟯, ideally ⟮stay up to date⟯ on ⟮current goverment information⟯ 
If you ⟮evacuate⟯, make sure you take ⟮c+;the necessary supplies&nbsp;⟯ 
If you decide to ⟮evacuate⟯ in response to an emergency, inform ⟮friends and family⟯ ⟮where you're going⟯ 
If you are subject to a severe storm/etc. and are staying home, ⟮fill up your bathtub with water⟯. It's not for ⟮drinking⟯, it's for ⟮flushing the toilet etc⟯. 
If you are subject to a severe storm/etc. and are staying home, perhaps the most important thing is to have ⟮water⟯ and ⟮non-perishable food⟯. 
Depending on the type of disaster and the government response, ⟮evacuation⟯ may be ⟮to predefined shelters⟯, but you also may be ⟮required or forced to find your own accomodation⟯ 
In general, in preparation for a storm, have at least ⟮3 l * 3 days⟯ of ⟮water⟯ per ⟮person⟯, ideally ⟮more⟯, plus ⟮some for washing hands etc.⟯ 
If you are staying home before a disaster hits, ⟮turn your fridge and freezer⟯ to ⟮the coldest setting⟯. Once ⟮the disaster hits⟯, ⟮keep them closed as much as possible⟯. This is to ⟮preserve the food as long as possible⟯. 
If you are in the path of a storm, ⟮fill up your gas tanks⟯, should you have a ⟮car⟯, so ⟮you can evacuate if necessary⟯. 
After&nbsp; a longer power outage, your ⟮food⟯ might be ⟮spoiled⟯. 
After a major catastrope you may ⟮be full of adrenaline⟯, so you might not ⟮notice huge injuries⟯ without ⟮checking yourself⟯.

## human geography

### heterotopia

⟮Heterotopia⟯ are ⟮similar to⟯ ⟮normal space⟯ in that they ⟮occupy space⟯, however they are ⟮different from⟯ ⟮normal space⟯ in a number of ways. 
In a very general sense, in ⟮heterotopia⟯ ⟮normal space⟯ is simultaneusly ⟮represented⟯, ⟮contested⟯ and ⟮inverted⟯ 
⟮Heterotopia⟯ often (related to their epistemic relation to real space) either ⟮create a space of illusory order⟯, or ⟮expose real space by contrast⟯ 
⟮Heterotopia⟯ are  often ⟮not accessible the way normal space is accessible⟯, they  either ⟮compell entrance (prisons etc.⟯) or ⟮require rituals/purification to enter⟯ ⟮Heterotopia⟯ have ⟮a function relative to society,⟯ amongst others ⟮heterotopia of crisis⟯ and  ⟮heterotopia of deviation⟯ 
Examples of ⟮heterotopia of crisis⟯ are ⟮boarding schools, honeymoon trips (for loss of virginity⟯) 
Examples of ⟮heterotopia of deviation⟯ are ⟮prisons, rest homes, psychiatric hospitals⟯ 
In modern society, ⟮heterotopia of crisis⟯ have the tendency to ⟮be replaced by heterotopia of deviation⟯ 
⟮Heterotopia⟯ have a close relationship with  the ⟮sacred (in a wide sense⟯) 
⟮Heterotopia⟯ often ⟮gather many different places in a single place⟯ (e.g.. gardens) ⟮Heterotopia⟯ often (related to time) correlate with ⟮heterochronia⟯, in that they either ⟮create a sort of achronia⟯ (e.g. ⟮museums⟯) or ⟮emphasize time in its most transitory⟯ (e.g. ⟮festivals⟯) 
All cultures have heterotopia, but ⟮the same heterotopia⟯ can have ⟮different functions⟯ ⟮in the same society at different times⟯, or ⟮in different societies⟯

##### capital cities

###### isolation and governance

According to <a href="https://www.nber.org/papers/w19028">Campante et al. 2013</a>, in ⟮authoritarian⟯ states ⟮capital cities⟯ being ⟮isolated⟯ correlates with ⟮worse quality of governance⟯ = ⟮more resource extraction (of the ruling elite from the country⟯). 
<a href="https://www.nber.org/papers/w19028">Campante et al. 2013</a> argue the relationship ⟮c+;capital cities being more isolated → worse quality of government⟯ is ⟮causal⟯, based on a more ⟮isolated⟯ ⟮capital city⟯ being ⟮less succeptible to revolution⟯.

# history

## theories & approaches

### historical progress & value

#### relativisim & determinism

⟮Historical relativism⟯ is the idea that ⟮a period of / event in history⟯ can only be ⟮understood in its own terms⟯. 
⟮Historical determinism⟯ is the idea that ⟮history is determined in some way, e.g. by rules⟯. 
⟮Historism⟯ and ⟮historicism⟯ may refer to ⟮historical relativism⟯, ⟮historical determinism⟯, an idea where ⟮history should be priviledged as a cause⟯ for ⟮society⟯, ⟮culture⟯, or t⟮he explanation of phenomena⟯. Thus ⟮these terms are confused to the point of unusability.⟯ 

#### presentism

In history, ⟮presentism⟯ is the ⟮judgement/interpretation⟯ of the ⟮past⟯ in terms of ⟮current⟯ ⟮values⟯ and ⟮concepts⟯ 

#### combination

##### historical determinism

⟮Whig history⟯ at its most narrow sees ⟮british history⟯ as the ⟮inevitable⟯ ⟮progress⟯ from ⟮dark and despotic arbitrary tyranny⟯ to ⟮enlightened british liberal democracy⟯. 
⟮Whig history⟯ inbetween the most narrow and the most wide definitions sees ⟮history⟯ as the ⟮inevitable⟯ ⟮progress⟯ from ⟮a dark and terrible past of unfreedom⟯ to ⟮a glorious present of freedom and democracy⟯. 
⟮Whig history⟯ at its most broad sees ⟮history⟯ as ⟮the inevitable march of progress (where progress depends on the context it is used in, the author, etc.⟯). 



### historiography

⟮Historiography⟯ is the study of ⟮the way we write (and have written) history.⟯ 
The ⟮historiography⟯ of a specific thing is ⟮how history has studied/seen a specific thing⟯ 

#### black legend

a ⟮black legend⟯ is a ⟮historiographical⟯ phenomenon where ⟮an overly negative portrayal of something⟯ is created, often through ⟮biased writing⟯ 


### scales

#### microhistory

⟮Microhistory⟯ is a genre of history that ⟮focuses on small units of research⟯ 
Charles Joyner defines the questions ⟮microhistory⟯ asks as ⟮Ask large questions in small places⟯ 
The things ⟮microhistory⟯ might focus on are things such as ⟮an event, community, individual (esp. an unimportant one⟯) 
The ⟮most famous work of microhistory⟯ is  ⟮The cheese and the worms⟯ by ⟮Carlo Ginzburg⟯ 

#### macrohistory

⟮macrohistory⟯ is the approach to history ⟮looking for large, long-term trends⟯ in world history 

#### annales-school

The terms ⟮longue durée, moyenne durée and événement⟯ for scales of analysing history by ⟮the annales-school⟯ were formulated in their current form in ⟮the 1950s⟯ by  ⟮⦗Fernand⦘ Braudel⟯ 
The three levels of history that ⟮the annales-school and fernand braudel⟯ distinguish are the ⟮longue durée⟯, ⟮moyenne durée⟯ and the ⟮événement⟯. 
The annales school favored ⟮the longue duree⟯ as a level of analysis 
Name|Timespan|Examples|Other names|Similar concepts
⟮c1,5-7;longue durée⟯|⟮c+;s3:7;hundreds or thousands of years⟯|⟮c+;s2:7;Changes in climatology or physical geography⟯||⟮c+;s2:7;Macrohistory⟯
⟮c8,11;moyenne durée⟯|⟮c+;s10:11;multiple years or decades⟯|⟮c+;s8:9;The great depression⟯

##### 万葉集

The ⟮　万葉集（まんようしゅう）⟯&nbsp;is ⟮the oldest⟯ ⟮collection of 和歌 poetry⟯ 
The ⟮　万葉集（まんようしゅう）⟯ was ⟮compiled in⟯ ⟮the latter half of the 700s (nara period⟯) 
The ⟮　万葉集（まんようしゅう）⟯&nbsp;is today considered ⟮a revered piece of literature⟯ 
While ⟮the  　万葉集（まんようしゅう） is from the latter 700s⟯, ⟮some of the poems⟯ are from ⟮far earlier (600ish⟯) 
the ⟮　万葉集（まんようしゅう）⟯&nbsp;contains ⟮4500ish⟯  和歌 poems

##### 3.11

⟮The 　東日本大震災（ひがしにほんだいしんさい） (amongst many other names⟯) was a disaster that hit ⟮japan⟯ on the ⟮11.03.2011⟯. 
The ⟮　東日本大震災（ひがしにほんだいしんさい）⟯ was caused by a ⟮9.0-9.1⟯ magnitude ⟮underwate earthquake⟯. 
The earthquake that caused the 　東日本大震災（ひがしにほんだいしんさい） subsequently caused ⟮a tsunami⟯ and ⟮nuclear meltdown⟯. ⟮The 　東日本大震災（ひがしにほんだいしんさい）⟯ cost ⟮around 200-400 billion 2018 US$⟯, making it ⟮the most costly natural disaster in history⟯. 
⟮　東日本大震災（ひがしにほんだいしんさい）⟯ is often just called ⟮3.11⟯

##### the economy

###### zaibatsu

⟮財閥⟯ ⟮controlled large parts of the japanese economy⟯ between ⟮the mid 明治 period⟯ and ⟮the end of ww2 (though in a sence, they still exist⟯) 

###### plaza accords

⟮the plaza accords⟯ were an agreement in ⟮1985⟯ that ⟮changed the fundamental relationship between⟯ ⟮Dollar, DM and Yen.⟯ 

###### bubble economy & lost decade

From ⟮the end of the war⟯ until ⟮the asset price crisis⟯, ⟮Japan⟯ experienced ⟮strong economic growth⟯. 
In ⟮pre-bubble⟯-era japan ⟮wealth was high⟯, employment was mostly for life and so ⟮conspicuous consumption was legion⟯ 

⟮バブル　景気（けいき）⟯is the japanese term for what we call ⟮the bubble economy/japanese asset price bubble.⟯. 
The ⟮バブル　景気（けいき）⟯ began in ⟮1986⟯, following ⟮the plaza accords⟯, and burst in ⟮1991⟯. 
The asset involved in ⟮the japanese asset price bubble⟯ was ⟮real estate⟯. 
⟮The lost decade⟯ is the english translation for the japanese ⟮失われた十年⟯. 
The ⟮japanese asset price bubble⟯ was followed by ⟮the lost decade/失われた十年⟯. 
Since ⟮the economy hasnt really recovered since the lost decade/失われた十年⟯, it is sometimes expanded to ⟮the lost 20/30 years.⟯ 
The ⟮employment ice age⟯ (ja⟮: 　就職氷河期（しゅうしょくひょうがき）⟯) was/is ⟮the period following the asset price bubble⟯ with ⟮unavailable or unstable employment⟯.  
The ⟮employment ice age⟯ strongly corrrelates with ⟮the lost decade(s).⟯

## structuralism

CLS studied myth from the perspective of structuralism.
⟮CLS⟯ elaborated his ⟮structuralist⟯ approach to myth in  『⟮The Structural Study of Myth⟯』 ⟮(c:4;1955⟯) 
The ⟮paradox of myth according⟯ to CLS is that ⟮anything can happen in a myth⟯, but that ⟮myths are similar across cultures⟯. 
According to CLS, ⟮myth⟯ is its own form of ⟮langue⟯, that is, its own ⟮structuralist system⟯ 
The ⟮langue⟯ of ⟮myth⟯ consists of ⟮mythemes⟯ . 
According to CLS, myths consist of ⟮pairs of⟯ ⟮mythemes⟯, generally ⟮one⟯ ⟮c+;s15;pair⟯ that is ⟮contradictiory/irreconcilable⟯, and ⟮one⟯ ⟮c+;s15;pair⟯ that is ⟮reconcilable⟯. 
The ⟮contradictory pair⟯ of mythemes ⟮is equated with⟯ ⟮the reconcilable pair,⟯ and so ⟮the contradiction is dissolved/solved⟯, e.g. ⟮life:death⟯ ⟮↔⟯ ⟮herbivore:hunter⟯ (which is ⟮reconcilable⟯ via the ⟮scavenger⟯); ⟮life:death⟯ ⟮↔⟯ ⟮sky:earth⟯ (which is ⟮reconcilable⟯ via ⟮mist⟯) 

Both ⟮mythical thought⟯ and ⟮science⟯ are ⟮equally⟯ ⟮sophisticated⟯ ⟮systems of thought⟯ according to ⟮CLS⟯, however, ⟮science⟯ is ⟮more effective at⟯ ⟮acquiring knowledge to harness the world⟯. 

Type of thought|Causality|Metaphor|Materials (physical)|Materials (thought)|Creates
⟮Mythical thought⟯|⟮Hypercausal (Everything must have a definite cause⟯)|⟮Bricolage⟯|⟮uses whatever is at hand⟯|⟮uses signs (which are limited⟯)|⟮creates structures⟯
⟮Scientific thought⟯|⟮Causal, but can accept uncertainty⟯|⟮Engineer⟯|⟮uses (within limit) the things it needs as materials⟯|⟮uses concepts (which can go beyond preexisting ideas⟯)|⟮creates changes in the world⟯


The fact that ⟮a granary killing a person⟯ is ⟮not explained by chance + granary being unstable⟯ but by ⟮witchcraft + granary being unstable⟯ is for CLS an example of ⟮the hyperneed for causality⟯ in ⟮mythical thought⟯ 
According to ⟮CLS⟯, in ⟮mythical thought⟯ ⟮intellectual drive⟯ leads to ⟮more categorization/discovery⟯, and ⟮need for causality⟯ makes these '⟮do something⟯'

#### narration

⟮Protofeminism⟯ is a term for ⟮activity or thought⟯ that is ⟮seen to be similar to feminism⟯, but ⟮before arrival of the movement itself⟯, i.e. ⟮before the 20th century⟯, or sometimes ⟮before the 18th century⟯ 

Mary Wollstonecraft lived in the 2nd half of the 1700s.
Mary Wollstonecraft is often called a proto-feminist, mainly due to her most well-known work 『A Vindication of the Rights of Woman』
Mary Wollstonecraft helped her sister Eliza to leave her husband and infant after (probably) postpartum depression, resulting in Eliza escaping the situation, but also in the social condemnation of her Mary 
Wollstonecraft had a close friendship with Jane Arden, valuing the intellectual atmosphere and also saying I have formed romantic notions of friendship ... I am a little singular in my thoughts of love and friendship; I must have the first place or none. 
In general, Mary Wollstonecrafts life was more popular a subject than her other writings (however some argue that this may have been overstated) until the advent of the feminist movement, then especially focusing on the 『Vindication of the Rights of Woman』 
As well as for her work, Mary Wollstonecraft is often admired for her unconventional personal relationships, which consisted of two affairs and then marrying a philosopher, after which she gave birth to Mary (Wollstonecraft) Shelley and then died due to complications

⟮Ada Lovelace⟯ was ⟮the only child⟯ of ⟮Lord Byron⟯ and ⟮the mathematician Lady Byron⟯ 
⟮Ada Lovelace⟯ was ⟮the wife⟯ of ⟮Charles Babbaage⟯

The ⟮Feminine Mystique⟯ is a book by ⟮Betty Friedan⟯ that is widely credited with ⟮sparking the beginning of second-wave feminism⟯ in the United States. 

⟮The Woman-Identified Woman⟯ (by ⟮Radicalesbians⟯) argues that ⟮heterosexuality⟯ necessarily leads to women ⟮serving/spending their time on⟯ and ⟮defining themselves by/as a negative to (in a Master-Slave dialectic sense))⟯ men (the whole package is called a "⟮man-identified woman⟯"), leaving her ⟮empty⟯. ⟮Lesbian feminism⟯ in general was ⟮a reaction to⟯ not ⟮being seen fully as a part of⟯ ⟮gay⟯ and ⟮feminist⟯ activism, perhaps most exemplefied by ⟮Betty Friedan⟯ calling them a "⟮lavender menace⟯" in ⟮1969⟯, triggering the ⟮1970⟯ ⟮interruption⟯ of ⟮the Congress to Unite Women⟯ by the ⟮Radicalesbians⟯ with their "⟮The Woman-Identified Woman⟯"

The ⟮Gender Identity Law⟯ (es: ⟮Ley de identidad de género⟯) (an ⟮Argentinian:: Location/jurisidiction⟯ law) allows a person ⟮to change⟯ their registered ⟮name⟯, ⟮image⟯, and ⟮sex specifier⟯ for ⟮free⟯ without ⟮a lawyer⟯. Additionally, ⟮hormone treatment⟯ and ⟮surgery⟯ do not ⟮require any kind of diganosis or referall letter⟯ and are made ⟮free⟯. It was promulgated in ⟮2012⟯ and is as of 2021 one of ⟮the most progressive transgender rights laws in existence⟯.

## trans

### confusion

⟮Transgender OCD⟯ consists of obsessive thoughts of the kind ⟮"What if I'm actually trans?" "What if I'm actually living a lie?"⟯ 

### identities

#### transvestism

⟮Transvestism⟯ is an ⟮older⟯ term (coined in ⟮the 1910s⟯) today ⟮more or less synonymous⟯ with ⟮cross-dressing⟯, although in the past ⟮it was also used for trans people (mirroring the general confusion about Transness⟯). 

#### travesti

The term ⟮travesti⟯ is used in ⟮Latin American⟯ countries—especially ⟮South American⟯—to designate people who were ⟮assigned male at birth⟯, but develop a gender identity according to different expressions of ⟮femininity⟯, 
⟮Travesti⟯ ⟮expression of femininity⟯ may be ⟮limited to cross-dressing⟯, or ⟮going whole hog with female names, cosmetic practises, hormone replacement therapy etc.⟯ 
⟮Travesti⟯ may ⟮identify⟯ as ⟮men or women⟯, more often they ⟮identify⟯ as ⟮a third gender⟯ or otherwise ⟮define their gender identity differently⟯. 
⟮Travesti⟯ generally face ⟮extreme marginalization⟯: Generally suffering ⟮childhood sexual abuse⟯, ⟮disowned from their families⟯ and ⟮removed from the education system⟯, with ⟮lack of access⟯ to ⟮housing⟯ and ⟮work⟯, they live in ⟮deficient housing⟯ ⟮(c:14;50⟯%) and often work ⟮as sex workers⟯ ⟮(c:14;70⟯%), often ⟮contracting HIV/AIDS⟯. 
⟮Travesti⟯ suffer from ⟮extremely high rates⟯ of ⟮hate crimes⟯ and ⟮murder⟯ as well as ⟮arbitrary detention⟯ up to ⟮torture⟯, and have a life expectancy of ⟮30 - 35⟯ years. 
⟮Often⟯, travesti ⟮use this term for themselves⟯ to ⟮highlight their marginalization as well as their gender identity⟯, nevertheless, the term is ⟮sometimes⟯ used ⟮as a translation of english terms such as transgender/trans/etc⟯, in these cases, it is often a ⟮slur⟯.

## nutrition

### practices

⟮Veganism⟯ is the practice of ⟮abstaining from the use of animal products⟯, particularly in ⟮diet⟯ 

### institutions

Abbreviation|Full
⟮EAR⟯|⟮Estimated Average Requirement⟯
⟮RDA⟯|⟮Recommended Dietary Allowance⟯

government organizations do not necessarily ⟮always agree⟯ on the ⟮amounts of nutrients⟯ needed to ⟮avoid deficiency⟯ or ⟮maximum amounts⟯ (however, ⟮the ballpark is often similar⟯ 
⟮RDAs⟯ are set higher than ⟮EARs⟯ to ⟮cover people with / situations where people have higher than average needs⟯. 
⟮chromium⟯ is designated an ⟮essential nutrient⟯ by the ⟮US⟯ and ⟮Japan⟯, but not the ⟮EU⟯ 
most health authorities recommend ⟮supplementing⟯ at least ⟮vitamin b12⟯ in ⟮vegan diets⟯

### antinutrients

⟮Antinutrients⟯ are ⟮compounds⟯ that ⟮interfere with the absorption of nutrients⟯. 
⟮Phytate (=phytic acid⟯), which may be found in ⟮whole grains⟯, ⟮nuts/seeds⟯, and ⟮legumes⟯, is an ⟮antinutrient⟯ which may ⟮inhibit⟯ ⟮iron⟯ and ⟮zinc⟯ ⟮absorption⟯ (also calcium to a lesser extent), even severely. 

### nutrients

A ⟮nutrient⟯ is a substance that an organism requires ⟮for good functioning / maintaining life⟯ 
⟮an essential nutrient⟯ is a nutrient that ⟮c+;cannot be synthesized by the body (=&gt; must be consumed for survival⟯) 
According to Marion Nestle, the ⟮list of essential nutrients⟯ is &nbsp;⟮almost certainly incomplete⟯ 
⟮Nutrient density⟯ is ⟮the ratio⟯ of ⟮nutrients⟯ to ⟮energy content⟯ 
An ⟮empty calorie⟯ is one is one gained from food that ⟮supplies energy⟯ but ⟮(basically) no other nutrients⟯ 
food ⟮fortification⟯ / ⟮enrichment⟯ is the process of ⟮adding micronutrients⟯ to food.

##### food energy

⟮Food energy⟯ is ⟮energy⟯ derived from ⟮food⟯ 
⟮Food energy⟯ is generally measured in ⟮kilocalories⟯ 
the ⟮calorie⟯, in its proper definition, is ⟮the amount of energy⟯&nbsp; ⟮needed to raise the temperature of one gram of water by one Kelvin / deg C⟯ 

Foods by food energy per mass:
1. ⟮fats⟯ 
2. ⟮alcohol(ethanol⟯) 
3. ⟮carbohydrates⟯/⟮protein⟯
⟮calorie⟯ is also sometimes called ⟮small calorie⟯, ⟮gram calorie⟯

###### carbs

⟮Saccharide⟯ is as far as I know a synonym for ⟮Carbohydrate⟯ 
Carbohydrates may be classified as ⟮monosaccharides⟯, ⟮disaccharides⟯ or ⟮polysaccharides⟯ 

###### protein

⟮Proteins⟯ are made up of ⟮amino acids⟯ 
⟮Amino acids⟯ are ⟮organic⟯ compounds that contain ⟮amino⟯ (–NH2) and ⟮carboxyl⟯ (–COOH) ⟮functional⟯ groups, along with a ⟮side chain⟯ ⟮(c:6;R⟯ group) ⟮specific to⟯ each ⟮amino acid⟯
The key elements of an ⟮amino acid⟯ are ⟮carbon⟯, ⟮hydrogen⟯, ⟮oxygen⟯, and ⟮nitrogen⟯, although the ⟮side chains⟯ sometimes ⟮include others⟯
of the ⟮21⟯ ⟮amino acids⟯ common to all life forms, for humans, ⟮nine⟯ are ⟮essential⟯, ⟮six⟯ are ⟮conditionally essential⟯ ⟮(c:19;synthesis may be limited by circumstances⟯), and ⟮six⟯ are ⟮non-essential⟯

###### fats

⟮Fat⟯ consists of (is an ester of, whatever that means) ⟮fatty acids⟯ 
⟮Fatty acids⟯ may either be ⟮saturated⟯ or ⟮unsaturated⟯. 
⟮Unsaturated⟯ ⟮fatty acids⟯ ones have ⟮one or more⟯ ⟮C=C double bonds⟯.⟮saturated⟯ factty acids have ⟮no⟯ ⟮C=C double bonds⟯. 
⟮Saturated fats⟯ are called that because they have the ⟮maximum possible number of hydrogen atoms⟯ attached to ⟮every carbon atom⟯, and thus no ⟮need (or possibility⟯) for ⟮double C=C bonds⟯ 
Unsaturated fats with ⟮one⟯ ⟮C=C double bond(s⟯) are called ⟮monounsaturated⟯ 
Unsaturated fats with ⟮two or more⟯ ⟮C=C double bond(s⟯) ⟮polyunsaturated⟯ 
Since ⟮cis⟯ and ⟮trans⟯ fats refer to the ⟮configuration of the molecule⟯ ⟮around the C=C double bond⟯, only ⟮unsaturated⟯ fats can be ⟮cis⟯ or ⟮trans⟯. 
since an ⟮unsaturated fatty acid⟯ can have ⟮more than one double bond⟯, it can be ⟮both cis and trans⟯ 
There are two ⟮essential⟯ fatty acids (EFAs) in human nutrition: ⟮alpha-linolenic acid⟯ (an ⟮omega-3 fatty acid⟯) and ⟮linoleic acid⟯ (an ⟮omega-6 fatty acid⟯). ⟮Other lipids needed by the body⟯ ⟮can be synthesized from these and other fats.⟯ 
Of the three main types of omega-3 fatty acids, ⟮plant foods⟯ typically contain ⟮only alpha-linolenic acid (ALA⟯). 

####### health

While the research is not completely settled, ⟮saturated⟯ and ⟮trans unsaturated fats⟯ are generally considered ⟮unhealthy⟯ and ⟮cis unsaturated fats⟯ considered ⟮healthy⟯

######### omega-3

There are three types of ⟮omega-3⟯ fatty acids, ⟮ALA⟯, ⟮EPA⟯, and ⟮DHA⟯.
of the ⟮three⟯ types of ⟮omega-3⟯ fatty acids relevant to human nutrition, only ⟮ALA⟯ is ⟮essential⟯.

Of the omega-3 fatty acids, only ⟮ALA⟯ is essential since ⟮EPA⟯ and ⟮DHA⟯ can be ⟮synthesized from the former⟯.
⟮EPA/DHA synthesis from ALA⟯ may be impaired in ⟮aging⟯ and generally is not a ⟮particularly efficient process⟯, as such, ⟮making sure to consume EPA/DHA⟯ is probably advisable.

#### micro

##### genesis

⟮Minerals⟯ ⟮end up in food⟯ via ⟮rock, soil, and water,⟯ which are ⟮absorbed by plants as they grow (or e.g. by animals licking rocks⟯), and then ⟮passed further up the food chain⟯. 
⟮What minerals we get⟯ from plants/animals depends on ⟮which they can store⟯, but also ⟮what the soil/animals further down the food chain had in them⟯. 
⟮Vitamins⟯ are ⟮biosynthesized⟯ by ⟮plants/animals⟯ and then ⟮absorbed by us⟯. 
⟮Which vitamins⟯ are ⟮contained in food⟯ depend on ⟮what the relevant vegetable or animal can biosythesize⟯. 

##### vitamins

Theoretically, all ⟮vitamins⟯ are defined to be ⟮essential nutrients⟯, however ⟮Vitamin D⟯ can be synthesized if ⟮exposed to sunlight (specifically UVB rays⟯), and is thus ⟮an exception⟯ 
Most ⟮vitamins⟯ are not ⟮single molecules⟯, but ⟮groups of related molecules⟯ which are each called ⟮vitamers⟯ 
Each ⟮vitamin⟯ is typically used in ⟮multiple reactions⟯, and therefore ⟮most have multiple functions⟯. 
⟮Vitamin B12⟯ is ⟮the only⟯ vitamin or nutrient ⟮not available from plant sources.⟯ 
⟮Vitamins⟯ are generally categorized as either ⟮water⟯- or ⟮fat⟯ soluble 
The ⟮water-soluble vitamins (the 8 B vitamins and vitamin C⟯) are ⟮easily excreted from the body⟯, thus generally ⟮hypervitaminosis (if referring to high storage levels⟯) is not an issue, therefore, however, ⟮consistent consumption is required⟯. 
Of the ⟮fat-soluble⟯ vitamins, ⟮A⟯ and ⟮D⟯ can ⟮accumulate in the body to a dangerous level⟯, resulting in ⟮hypervitaminosis⟯ 
⟮Hypervitaminosis⟯ is a condition of abnormally high ⟮storage levels (most of the time, although some sources also just use it for any overdose⟯) of ⟮vitamins⟯ 
In general, ⟮excess intake⟯ of vitamins is ⟮unlikely⟯ from ⟮food sources⟯, but possible via ⟮supplements⟯ 



Common Designation|More Properly
⟮Vitamin C⟯|⟮Ascorbic acid⟯


###### vitamin D

⟮Vitamin D⟯ deficiency is common worldwide, occurring in about a ⟮1 billion⟯ people, and is surprisingly common in ⟮europe⟯. 
⟮Vitamin D⟯ has many function, amongst the most important being ⟮increasing the absorption⟯ of ⟮minerals⟯ such as ⟮calcium, magnesium, and phosphate⟯ 
⟮Vitamin D⟯ ⟮deficiency⟯ can cause or excarbate a wide variety of conditions, many featuring ⟮bone⟯ or ⟮muscle⟯ ⟮weakness⟯, and some ⟮respiratiory infections⟯ as well as ⟮mental illnesses⟯ 
⟮Rickets⟯ is a condition that results in ⟮weak⟯ and ⟮soft⟯ ⟮bones⟯ in ⟮children⟯ 

⟮Vitamin D⟯ occurs only ⟮rarely⟯ in food, specifically in ⟮oily fish⟯ such as ⟮salmon/herring/mackerel⟯ as well as ⟮mushrooms⟯ (if these have been ⟮exposed to UVB ligh ≈ been in the sun⟯) 
While ⟮Vitamin D⟯ can be synthesized by contact with ⟮UVB⟯ rays from ⟮sunlight⟯, many people ⟮do not do this enough⟯, it is not necessarily possible in ⟮winter⟯, and may not be ⟮advisable⟯ due to ⟮skin cancer risk⟯ 
In the ⟮US⟯ and some other countries, ⟮milk⟯ and ⟮milk substitutes⟯ as well as ⟮breakfast cereals⟯ are fortified with ⟮Vitamin D⟯.

###### iron

⟮Iron⟯ ⟮from food⟯ comes in two forms, ⟮heme⟯ ⟮iron⟯ which can only be gotten from ⟮animal flesh⟯, and ⟮non-heme⟯ ⟮iron⟯ which can also be gotten from ⟮other sources⟯

###### iodine

⟮Iodine⟯ ⟮deficiency⟯ leads to many bad outcomes, many due to the fact that ⟮iodine⟯ is necessary to ⟮make one of the thyroid hormones (thyroxine⟯), lack of which leads to ⟮high levels of thyroid stimulating hormone (TSH⟯), causing ⟮the thyroid to swell⟯ 
in many countries, ⟮table salt⟯ is fortified with ⟮iodine⟯ in the form of ⟮iodine⟯ ⟮salts⟯, which has proved effective at preventing ⟮iodine⟯ ⟮deficiency⟯ 
In Deutschland kommen Iodverbindungen ⟮in den Böden⟯ unverändert ⟮in zu geringen Mengen vor⟯. Durch ⟮die Iodierung von Speisesalz⟯ ist ⟮der Iodmangel in Deutschland verringert worden⟯, dennoch besteht in Deutschland bei ⟮36⟯ % der Bevölkerung ein ⟮milder⟯ und bei ⟮21⟯ % ein ⟮moderater bis schwerer⟯ ⟮Iodmangel⟯. 

##### choline

###### testicular self-examination

General medical advice is to self-examine ⟮one's testicles⟯ ⟮once a month⟯ to check for ⟮testicular cancer.⟯ 
When ⟮self-examining ones testicles,⟯ the two parts are to ⟮feel them once-over⟯ and to ⟮measure them⟯. 
When measuring your testicles, having ⟮one testicle be larger than the other⟯ is ⟮normal⟯, what you want to look out for is ⟮size changes diachronically⟯. 
When ⟮feeling your testicles⟯, you're feeling for ⟮lumps/bumps or similar⟯, representing ⟮possible tumors⟯. Generally, these are ⟮painless⟯, though ⟮a dull soreness⟯ in ⟮your whole testicles⟯ is also ⟮something to keep an eye out for⟯. 
If you f⟮ind  something unusal⟯ during testicular self-examination, ⟮consult with your doctor⟯.

###### reproduction

####### pearl index

⟮The pearl index⟯ is a method for reporting ⟮the effectiveness of a birth control method.⟯ 
The pearl index is ⟮the number of pregnancies⟯ per ⟮100⟯ that ⟮the women participating in the trial⟯ experienced in ⟮a year⟯. 
Specifically, the ⟮pearl index⟯ is calculated via ⟮(⟯⟮n_pregnancies⟯ ⟮* 12 * 100⟯⟮) / (⟯⟮n_women⟯ ⟮*⟯ ⟮n_months⟯⟮c10;⟯) 
It is theoretically possible to recieve a pearl index of ⟮1200⟯ if ⟮all women get pregnant in the first month⟯, however the pearl index may serve as t⟮he risk of getting pregnant in a year⟯ if ⟮the pregnancy rate is relatively low⟯ 
⟮Pearl indices⟯ ⟮vary⟯ based on ⟮the culture/demographics⟯ of the people being studied, and ⟮the method of instruction⟯. 
More seriously, since ⟮the most fertile couples⟯ will ⟮get pregnant first⟯, and since ⟮people will get better at using a contraceptive method as time goes on⟯, ⟮the longer⟯ ⟮a study runs⟯, ⟮the lower⟯ ⟮its reported Pearl index will be⟯. 
⟮Typical-use pearl index⟯|⟮pearl index including occasional mistakes etc.⟯
⟮perfect-use pearl index⟯|⟮pearl index under ideal usage⟯

Method of birth control|Perfect-use pearl index|Typical-use pearl index
⟮the Pill⟯|⟮0.2-0.5⟯|⟮5-10⟯
⟮Male condom⟯|⟮~2⟯|⟮10-20⟯
⟮Pulling out⟯|⟮~5⟯|⟮~20⟯

###### constituent parts

flex-container:✫Schematic_diagram_of_the_human_eye.svg✫


The ⟮cornea⟯ is ⟮the transparent front part of the eye⟯ that ⟮covers⟯ the ⟮iris⟯, ⟮pupil⟯, and ⟮anterior chamber⟯. 
The ⟮anterior chamber⟯ is the part of the eye ⟮between⟯ ⟮the iris⟯ and ⟮the cornea⟯ filled with ⟮aqueous humour⟯. 
The ⟮iris⟯ controls the eyes ⟮aperture⟯ = ⟮the size of the pupil⟯. 
The ⟮iris⟯ is the thing that determines ⟮eye color⟯. 
The ⟮pupil⟯ is a ⟮hole⟯ through which ⟮light travels into the eye⟯. 
The ⟮lens⟯ of the eye sits ⟮behind the iris/pupil⟯. 
The ⟮vitreous body/humor⟯ is ⟮a clear gel⟯. 
The ⟮optic nerve⟯ ⟮connects the brain to the eye⟯. 
We ⟮can't see anything⟯ at ⟮the blind spot⟯, since ⟮we lack photoreceptor cells there⟯. 
We require a ⟮blind spot⟯ because ⟮the optic nerve attaches there⟯. 
Our brain ⟮interpolates⟯ ⟮the blind spot⟯ and ⟮fills it in⟯. 
The ⟮retina⟯ is the  tissue ⟮covering⟯ much of ⟮the inner part of the eye⟯ and ⟮transforms⟯ ⟮light⟯ into ⟮neural signals⟯ via ⟮the photoreceptor cells⟯ ⟮contained within⟯. 
⟮Photoreceptor cells⟯ ⟮transform light into biological signals.⟯ 

flex-container:✫sm_1280px-Blausen_0390_EyeAnatomy_Sectional.jpg✫


The ⟮anterior chamber of the eyeball⟯ lies ⟮between⟯ ⟮the cornea⟯ and ⟮the iris/pupil⟯ 
The ⟮posterior chamber of the eyeball⟯ is ⟮the narrow space between⟯ ⟮the iris⟯ and ⟮the suspensory ligament of the lens⟯ 
the ⟮aqueous humor⟯ and the ⟮vitreous humor⟯ are ⟮not the same thing⟯ 
The ⟮anterior chamber of the eyeball⟯ is ⟮filled with⟯ ⟮aqueous humor⟯ 
The ⟮space that fills most of they eye behind the lens⟯ is ⟮filled by⟯ ⟮vitreous body/humor⟯ 
the ⟮aqueous humour⟯ is ⟮more fluid⟯ than the ⟮vitreous humor⟯ 

The ⟮white of the eye⟯ is more properly called the ⟮sclera⟯. 
^This term 'sclera
The ⟮sclera⟯ ⟮protecc⟯ your eye 

The ⟮optic disc⟯ is the area where t⟮he optic nerve 'enters'/connects with the eye⟯ 
The ⟮choroid⟯ is ⟮the layer providing nourishment⟯ to the eye

###### photoreceptor cells

Our eyes have two kinds of ⟮photoreceptor cells⟯, ⟮rods⟯ and ⟮cones⟯. 
⟮ All photoreceptor cells, i.e. cones and rods,⟯ lie in ⟮the retina⟯. 

name|can see|occur where
⟮cones⟯|⟮color⟯|⟮c+;the macula ＆ fovea⟯
⟮rods⟯|⟮B/W⟯|⟮everywhere but the macula/fovea⟯

⟮h∞;[[[✫sm_220px-Distribution_of_Cones_and_Rods_on_Human_Retina.png✫✫sm_paste-bc785a2d511b7575efe441c3d7bedcfd03c9b3b1.jpg✫✫sm_paste-cfa41a06701fe6c68f1d7d8592c680ace8096dee.jpg✫✫sm_main-qimg-2eeba96357696c0a7a943ecf31157b2d.webp✫]]]⟯
We distinguish ⟮3 types⟯ of ⟮cones⟯, based on ⟮what wavelength light they respond to⟯ 
we normally say  we have ⟮cones⟯ for ⟮each of the R, G, B colors⟯, but in fact, we have ⟮one for blue⟯ and ⟮two which are very similar, both detecting green/yellow/red,⟯ only with ⟮one shifted slightly⟯. 
Since ⟮cone sensitivity⟯ is ⟮a curve⟯, ⟮cones for a given color/wavelength⟯ also ⟮percieve other colors/wavelengths⟯&nbsp; Due to ⟮the two cones that both have peaks close to it⟯, we are very good at percieving ⟮shades of green⟯. 
We can see something like ⟮1-10 million⟯ ⟮different colors⟯, though the exact amount is controversial 
⟮Visible spectrum⟯: ⟮380⟯ - ⟮750⟯ ⟮nm⟯ (though it varies somewhat depending on the human)

##### appendix & appendicitis

⟮Appendicitis⟯ is the english and formally correct name for ⟮Blinddarmentzündung⟯. 
Der ⟮Wurmfortsatz des Blinddarms⟯ is the german name for ⟮appendix⟯. 
⟮appendicitis⟯ is ⟮inflammation of⟯ ⟮the appendix⟯ 
⟮The inflammation of the appendix that is appendicitis⟯ is often caused by ⟮a blockage of the appendix⟯. 
⟮The blockage of the appendix leading to appendicitis⟯ is most commonly due to ⟮a calcified 'stone' made of feces⟯. Due to this reason, ⟮a high-fiber diet⟯ reduces ⟮the long-term risk of appendicitis⟯. 
The risk of getting ⟮appendicitis⟯ at some point in your life is ⟮7-8⟯%, most commonly in ⟮those under 30⟯. 
There is ⟮c+;a cluster ofsigns＆symptoms⟯ associated with ⟮appendicitis⟯, but ⟮a fairly high percentage (40%⟯) ⟮do not present most/all of the typical symptoms⟯, and ⟮a smaller percentage⟯ may have ⟮their pain be located differently⟯, due to ⟮the appendix itself being in a different location⟯. 
The ⟮pain⟯ for ⟮appendicitis⟯ generally appears relatively ⟮suddenly⟯ ⟮near or above the belly button⟯, ⟮from where it then moves⟯ ⟮into the right lower belly⟯. 
The ⟮pain⟯ for ⟮appendicitis⟯ is usually ⟮strong⟯ and ⟮gets worse⟯ with ⟮(esp. jarring) movement⟯ (one can test for this by ⟮jumping up or down⟯). 
⟮The hamburger sign⟯ is used for ⟮assessing if the person has appendicitis⟯: the person ⟮is asked if they would like to eat their favorite food⟯: A ⟮positive hamburger sign⟯ is ⟮them declining the food⟯, with ⟮a sensitivity (true postive rate⟯) of ⟮80%⟯. 
One of the symptoms of ⟮appendicitis⟯ is ⟮stiff⟯ ⟮belly muscles⟯. Connected to this is ⟮rebound tenderness⟯: If you ⟮press on your belly for a while⟯, and then ⟮suddenly release⟯, the ⟮main pain⟯ will be ⟮on release (though the initial press will also hurt⟯) 
To determine the exisitence of ⟮appendicitis⟯ with certainty, ⟮CT/ultrasound⟯ is needed. 
If ⟮appendicitis is left untreated⟯, ⟮the appendix may burst⟯, causing ⟮the inflammation of the interior abdominal wall (peritonitis⟯). Once this has happened, there is ⟮a high risk of death⟯. 
⟮Appendicitis⟯ is also often accomodated by ⟮nausea⟯ and ⟮vomiting⟯.

##### thrombi

What we call ⟮a blood clot⟯ is more properly called ⟮thrombus⟯. 
⟮Thrombosis⟯ is the formation of ⟮a thrombus⟯ ⟮inside a blood vessel⟯ that ⟮obstructs the flow of blood⟯. 
If ⟮the thrombus then breaks free⟯, it becomes an ⟮embolus⟯. 
if ⟮a thrombus⟯, after ⟮breaking free and becoming an embolus⟯ ⟮get lodged again⟯, that is a form ⟮embolism⟯, specifically a ⟮s10;⟮thromboembolism⟯⟯. 
Based on where they occur, ⟮thromboses⟯ are classified into ⟮venous⟯ and ⟮arterial⟯. 

###### thromboses

⟮Thromboses⟯ most often occur in ⟮the veins⟯ of ⟮the legs⟯, other bodyparts are ⟮affected far more rarely⟯. 
If you have a ⟮thrombosis⟯, your limb, esp. your leg, will often become some of the following:&nbsp; ⟮swollen::size⟯, ⟮turn red or blue::color⟯, ⟮have more visible veins::appearance of thing⟯, ⟮be shiny due to taut::surface appearance⟯, ⟮may feel more heavy::feeling⟯, ⟮be warmer::feeling⟯, and ⟮hurt quite severely, especially while stepping::pain⟯. 
⟮Thromboses⟯ are often caused by ⟮not moving that bodypart for a while⟯, e.g. ⟮because of an illness that prevents it⟯ 
If ⟮a thrombus blocks the blood vessel completely⟯, ⟮the surrounding tissue will start to die⟯.

##### emboli

An ⟮embolus⟯ is ⟮some kind of foreign object⟯ (amongst others: ⟮s1:2;⟮thrombi⟯, ⟮fat globules, gas bubbles, cholesterol plaque/crystals⟯⟯ ) that ⟮travels through the bloodstream⟯. 
If an ⟮embolus⟯ ⟮gets lodged⟯ and thus ⟮s6;⟮blocks a blood vessel⟯⟯, this is known as an ⟮embolism⟯. ⟮s6:8;If it's ⟮a thrombus⟯ that ⟮gets lodged⟯, it is known as a ⟮thromboembolism⟯.⟯ 

###### pulmonary embolism

The ⟮severity of the effects⟯ of ⟮an embolism⟯ vary based on ⟮location (in the body⟯). 
One of ⟮the most severe types⟯ of embolism is ⟮pulmonary embolism⟯, i.e. ⟮embolism in the lungs⟯. 
If ⟮a pulmonary embolism⟯ ⟮is caused by thrombosis⟯ (as is ⟮common⟯), it is known as ⟮a pulmonary thromboembolism⟯. 
On average, the risk of experiencing a ⟮pulmonary embolism⟯ is ⟮0.1% in one year⟯, the ⟮mortality⟯ is ⟮10%⟯. It ⟮gets more common⟯ with ⟮age⟯.

### disorder

A disorder is some sort of disruption of regular functions with recgonizable signs and symptoms with no statement of if we know the cause, if there are multiple causes, etc.

#### nervous system

##### dystonia

»Dystonia« is a neurological ＿disorder＿ where muscle contractions cause jerking, twitching, and abnormal movements.

Benign Essential Blepharospasm is a focal dystonia of the eye muscle.
Most eyelid twitching is most common idiopathic.
Rarely, eyelid twitching is a symptom of benign essential blepharospasm or a serious neurological disorder.

### syndrome

the distinction between parts of TBDSSMFM ⟮may be more vague⟯ ⟮in other communities⟯, especially in ⟮queer and fandom communities⟯.
As of 2021, ⟮all the TBDSSMFM identities⟯ are often ⟮conflated into a giant superidentity⟯, most often ⟮just called top/bottom⟯ or ⟮masc(or butch)/femme⟯.
This ⟮top/bottom superidentity⟯ is then most often also seen as having ⟮a scope⟯ of ⟮many/all aspects of ones life⟯, ⟮a duration⟯ of ⟮one's whole life⟯, and ⟮an identity that is essentialized⟯. This seems to me basically like ⟮the hegemonic (= mainstream) idea of masculinity⟯.
This is also supported by the fact that ⟮people⟯ (or ⟮fictional characters⟯) who ⟮subscribe to the top/bottom superidentity⟯ also ⟮then engage in misogynist/sexist practices/beliefs/etc.⟯

# isms

## phenomena

### violence

#### behavioral and structural

⟮Structural violence⟯ is ⟮the tangible harm⟯ done by ⟮social structures⟯. 
⟮behavioral violence⟯ is ⟮the tangible harm⟯ done ⟮by agents volitionally⟯ 
⟮Structural violence⟯ is contrasted with ⟮behavioral violence⟯. 
However, in a second analysis, we might argue that ⟮we can reduce behavioral violence to structural violence⟯, for example via the concepts of ⟮stochastic/scripted terrorism/violence⟯ 
⟮about 300k deaths in a year in the US are attributable to income inequality⟯ is an example of ⟮structural violence⟯ 
⟮"Over 20 years’ worth of studies show that people of color who arrive at a hospital while having a heart attack are significantly less likely to receive aspirin, beta-blocking drugs, clot-dissolving drugs, acute cardiac catheterization, angioplasty, or bypass surgery."⟯ is an example of ⟮structural violence⟯ 

##### stochastic violence

⟮Stochastic violence⟯ is the idea of ⟮spreading a violent message via mass communication⟯, which ⟮makes resulting acts of violence⟯ ⟮probable⟯, but ⟮the actual place and time of the occurrence are⟯ ⟮random⟯. 
⟮Stochastic violence⟯ used for ⟮terrorism⟯ is called ⟮stochastic terrorism⟯ 
By ⟮transforming⟯ ⟮lone wolves⟯ ⟮into⟯ ⟮instances of larger patterns of violent messages⟯, ⟮stochastic violence⟯ ⟮unmasks behavioural violence⟯ as ⟮structural violence⟯ (perhaps) 
⟮Scripted violence⟯ is where ⟮someone describes the kind of violence they want to be carried out⟯, and then ⟮leaves it up to the audience to carry out this violence⟯. It may be seen as an instace of ⟮attempted stochastic violence/terrorism⟯
